<tr>
<th scope="row">Signature</th>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg" title="Margaret Thatcher's signature"><img alt="" data-file-height="269" data-file-width="957" height="36" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg/128px-Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg/192px-Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg/256px-Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg.png 2x" width="128"/></a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Margaret Hilda Thatcher, Baroness Thatcher</b>, <span style="font-size:100%;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lady_of_the_Order_of_the_Garter" title="Lady of the Order of the Garter">LG</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">PC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Institute_of_Chemistry" title="Fellow of the Royal Institute of Chemistry">FRIC</a></span> (<a href="/wiki/Given_name#Name_at_birth" title="Given name">née</a> <b>Roberts</b>; 13 October 1925 – 8 April 2013) was a British <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/stateswoman" title="wikt:stateswoman">stateswoman</a> who was the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> from 1979 to 1990 and the <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Conservative Party (UK)">Leader</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> from 1975 to 1990. She was the longest-serving British prime minister of the 20th century, and the first woman to have held the office. A Soviet journalist dubbed her <b>The Iron Lady</b>, a nickname that became associated with her uncompromising politics and leadership style. As Prime Minister, she implemented policies that have come to be known as <a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a>.</p>
<p>A research <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a> before becoming a <a href="/wiki/Barrister" title="Barrister">barrister</a>, Thatcher was <a href="/wiki/List_of_MPs_elected_in_the_United_Kingdom_general_election,_1959" title="List of MPs elected in the United Kingdom general election, 1959">elected Member of Parliament</a> for <a href="/wiki/Finchley_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Finchley (UK Parliament constituency)">Finchley</a> in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1959" title="United Kingdom general election, 1959">1959</a>. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> appointed her <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Education" title="Secretary of State for Education">Secretary of State for Education and Science</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Heath_ministry" title="Heath ministry">1970 government</a>. In 1975, Thatcher defeated Heath in the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1975" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1975">Conservative Party leadership election</a> to become <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a> and became the first woman to lead a major political party in the United Kingdom. She became Prime Minister after winning the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">1979 general election</a>.</p>
<p>On moving into <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/10_Downing_Street" title="10 Downing Street">10 Downing Street</a></span>, Thatcher introduced a series of political and economic initiatives intended to reverse high unemployment and Britain's struggles in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a> and an ongoing recession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[nb 1]</a></sup> Her political philosophy and economic policies emphasised <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulation</a> (particularly of the financial sector), flexible labour markets, the privatisation of <a href="/wiki/State-owned_enterprise" title="State-owned enterprise">state-owned companies</a>, and reducing the power and influence of trade unions. Thatcher's popularity during her first years in office waned amid recession and high unemployment, until victory in the 1982 <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a> and the recovering economy brought a resurgence of support, resulting in her <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">re-election in 1983</a>. She narrowly escaped an <a href="/wiki/Brighton_hotel_bombing" title="Brighton hotel bombing">assassination attempt in 1984</a>.</p>
<p>Thatcher was re-elected for a third term in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1987" title="United Kingdom general election, 1987">1987</a>. During this period her support for a <a href="/wiki/Community_Charge" title="Community Charge">Community Charge</a> (referred to as the "poll tax") was widely unpopular, and her views on the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Community</a> were not shared by others in her Cabinet. She resigned as Prime Minister and party leader in November 1990, after <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a> launched a <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1990" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1990">challenge to her leadership</a>. After retiring from the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">Commons</a> in 1992, she was given a <a href="/wiki/Life_peer" title="Life peer">life peerage</a> as Baroness Thatcher (of <a href="/wiki/Kesteven" title="Kesteven">Kesteven</a> in the County of <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire" title="Lincolnshire">Lincolnshire</a>) which entitled her to sit in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>. After a series of small strokes in 2002, she was advised to withdraw from public speaking. Despite this, she managed to pre-record a eulogy to <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> prior to his death, which was broadcast at <a href="/wiki/Death_and_state_funeral_of_Ronald_Reagan" title="Death and state funeral of Ronald Reagan">his funeral</a> in 2004. In 2013, <a href="/wiki/Death_and_funeral_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Death and funeral of Margaret Thatcher">she died of another stroke</a> in London, at the age of 87. She is regarded as a controversial yet epochal figure of British politics, and arguments over the ramifications of Thatcherism persist.</p>

<ul>

<p><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher"><img alt="Signature of Margaret Thatcher.svg" data-file-height="269" data-file-width="957" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg/120px-Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg/180px-Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg/240px-Signature_of_Margaret_Thatcher.svg.png 2x" width="120"/></a></p>
<a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom"><img alt="Royal Coat of Arms of the United Kingdom (HM Government).svg" data-file-height="1256" data-file-width="1530" height="49" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom_%28HM_Government%29.svg/60px-Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom_%28HM_Government%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom_%28HM_Government%29.svg/90px-Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom_%28HM_Government%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom_%28HM_Government%29.svg/120px-Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom_%28HM_Government%29.svg.png 2x" width="60"/></a></td>

<li class="nv-talk"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Margaret_Thatcher_sidebar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Template talk:Margaret Thatcher sidebar (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Margaret_Thatcher_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>

<p>Thatcher was born Margaret Hilda Roberts on 13 October 1925, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grantham,_Lincolnshire" title="Grantham, Lincolnshire">Grantham, Lincolnshire</a>. Her father was <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Roberts" title="Alfred Roberts">Alfred Roberts</a>, originally from Northamptonshire, and her mother was Beatrice Ethel (née Stephenson) from Lincolnshire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20061_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20061-3">[2]</a></sup> She spent her childhood in Grantham, where her father owned two grocery shops. She and her older sister Muriel (1921–2004) were raised in the flat above the larger of the two, on North Parade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20063_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20063-4">[3]</a></sup> In 1938 the Roberts family gave sanctuary to a teenage Jewish girl escaping <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell201138.E2.80.9339_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell201138.E2.80.9339-5">[4]</a></sup> Margaret described this in her memoirs as among the significant achievements of her formative years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her father was active in local politics and the <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodist</a> church, serving as an <a href="/wiki/Alderman" title="Alderman">alderman</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Methodist_local_preacher" title="Methodist local preacher">local preacher</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20068_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20068-7">[6]</a></sup> and brought up his daughter as a strict <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_Methodist_Church_(Great_Britain)" title="Wesleyan Methodist Church (Great Britain)">Wesleyan Methodist</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-8">[7]</a></sup> attending the <a href="/wiki/Finkin_Street_Methodist_Church" title="Finkin Street Methodist Church">Finkin Street Methodist Church</a>. He came from a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> family but stood – as was then customary in local government – as an <a href="/wiki/Independent_politician" title="Independent politician">Independent</a>. He was Mayor of Grantham in 1945–46 and lost his position as alderman in 1952 after the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> won its first majority on Grantham Council in 1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20068_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20068-7">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Margaret Roberts attended Huntingtower Road Primary School and won a scholarship to <a href="/wiki/Kesteven_and_Grantham_Girls%27_School" title="Kesteven and Grantham Girls' School">Kesteven and Grantham Girls' School</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20065_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20065-9">[8]</a></sup> Her school reports showed hard work and continual improvement; her extracurricular activities included the piano, field hockey, poetry recitals, swimming and walking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20066_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20066-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200821.E2.80.9322_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200821.E2.80.9322-11">[10]</a></sup> She was <a href="/wiki/Head_girl_and_head_boy" title="Head girl and head boy">head girl</a> in 1942–43.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> In her <a href="/wiki/Sixth_form" title="Sixth form">upper sixth year</a> she applied for a scholarship to study chemistry at <a href="/wiki/Somerville_College,_Oxford" title="Somerville College, Oxford">Somerville College, Oxford</a>, but she was initially rejected and was offered a place only after another candidate withdrew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200612_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200612-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200823_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200823-14">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Roberts arrived at Oxford in 1943 and graduated in 1947 with <a href="/wiki/British_undergraduate_degree_classification" title="British undergraduate degree classification">Second-Class Honours</a> in the four-year Chemistry Bachelor of Science degree, specialising in <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a> under the supervision of <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Hodgkin" title="Dorothy Hodgkin">Dorothy Hodgkin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200825.E2.80.9327_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200825.E2.80.9327-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200616_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200616-16">[15]</a></sup> Her dissertation was on the structure of the antibiotic <a href="/wiki/Gramicidin" title="Gramicidin">gramicidin</a>. Even while working on chemistry, she was already thinking towards law and politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lecher20130408_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-lecher20130408-17">[16]</a></sup> She was reportedly more proud of becoming the first Prime Minister with a science degree than the first female Prime Minister,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-runciman20130606_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-runciman20130606-18">[17]</a></sup> and as Prime Minister attempted to preserve Somerville as a <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_college" title="Women's college">women's college</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bowcott20161230_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-bowcott20161230-19">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Roberts became President of the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Conservative_Association" title="Oxford University Conservative Association">Oxford University Conservative Association</a> in 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200620.E2.80.9321_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200620.E2.80.9321-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200828_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200828-21">[20]</a></sup> She was influenced at university by political works such as <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Road_to_Serfdom" title="The Road to Serfdom">The Road to Serfdom</a></i> (1944),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200830_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200830-22">[21]</a></sup> which condemned economic intervention by government as a precursor to an authoritarian state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200317_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200317-23">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postgraduate_career:_1947.E2.80.931951">Postgraduate career: 1947–1951</span></h3>
<p>After graduating, Roberts moved to <a href="/wiki/Colchester" title="Colchester">Colchester</a> in Essex to work as a research chemist for <a href="/wiki/BX_Plastics" title="BX Plastics">BX Plastics</a> near <a href="/wiki/Manningtree" title="Manningtree">Manningtree</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200617_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200617-24">[23]</a></sup> In 1948 she applied for a job at <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chemical_Industries" title="Imperial Chemical Industries">ICI</a>, but was rejected after the personnel department assessed her as "headstrong, obstinate and dangerously self-opinionated".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> Jon Agar explores her career in chemistry and argues that her understanding of modern scientific research impacted her views as Prime Minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Roberts joined the local Conservative Association and attended the party conference at <a href="/wiki/Llandudno" title="Llandudno">Llandudno</a> in 1948, as a representative of the University Graduate Conservative Association.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622-27">[26]</a></sup> One of her Oxford friends was also a friend of the Chair of the <a href="/wiki/Dartford" title="Dartford">Dartford</a> Conservative Association in <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>, who were looking for candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622-27">[26]</a></sup> Officials of the association were so impressed by her that they asked her to apply, even though she was not on the Conservative Party's approved list: she was selected in January 1951, aged 25, and added to the approved list <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/post_ante" title="wikt:post ante">post ante</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200836_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200836-28">[27]</a></sup> At a dinner following her formal adoption as Conservative candidate for Dartford in February 1951 she met <a href="/wiki/Denis_Thatcher" title="Denis Thatcher">Denis Thatcher</a>, a successful and wealthy divorced businessman, who drove her to her Essex train.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200836_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200836-28">[27]</a></sup> In preparation for the election Roberts moved to Dartford, where she supported herself by working as a research chemist for <a href="/wiki/J._Lyons_and_Co." title="J. Lyons and Co.">J. Lyons and Co.</a> in Hammersmith, part of a team developing <a href="/wiki/Emulsion" title="Emulsion">emulsifiers</a> for <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream" title="Ice cream">ice cream</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ns-rs_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ns-rs-29">[28]</a></sup> Shortly after her marriage, she and her husband began attending <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Anglican</a> services and would later convert to <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belz_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belz-30">[29]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Filby_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Filby-31">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career">Early political career</span></h2>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1950" title="United Kingdom general election, 1950">1950</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1951" title="United Kingdom general election, 1951">1951 general elections</a>, Roberts was the Conservative candidate for the safe Labour seat of <a href="/wiki/Dartford_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Dartford (UK Parliament constituency)">Dartford</a>. The local party selected her as its candidate because, though not a dynamic public speaker, Roberts was well-prepared and fearless in her answers; another prospective candidate recalled that "Once she opened her mouth, the rest of us began to look rather second-rate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-runciman20130606_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-runciman20130606-18">[17]</a></sup> She attracted media attention as the youngest and the only female candidate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623.E2.80.9324_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623.E2.80.9324-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200837_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200837-33">[32]</a></sup> She lost on both occasions to <a href="/wiki/Norman_Dodds" title="Norman Dodds">Norman Dodds</a>, but reduced the Labour majority by 6,000, and then a further 1,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623.E2.80.9324_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623.E2.80.9324-32">[31]</a></sup> During the campaigns, she was supported by her parents and by Denis Thatcher, whom she married in December 1951.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623.E2.80.9324_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623.E2.80.9324-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denis_Thatcher_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denis_Thatcher-34">[33]</a></sup> Denis funded his wife's studies for the <a href="/wiki/Bar_association" title="Bar association">bar</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200625_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200625-35">[34]</a></sup> she qualified as a barrister in 1953 and specialised in taxation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200835_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200835-36">[35]</a></sup> Later that same year their twins <a href="/wiki/Carol_Thatcher" title="Carol Thatcher">Carol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Thatcher" title="Mark Thatcher">Mark</a> were born, delivered prematurely by <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">Caesarean section</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200626_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200626-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_Parliament:_1959.E2.80.931970">Member of Parliament: 1959–1970</span></h3>
<p>In 1954, Thatcher was defeated when she sought selection to be the Conservative party candidate for the <a href="/wiki/Orpington_by-election,_1955" title="Orpington by-election, 1955">Orpington by-election</a> of January 1955. She chose not to stand as a candidate in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1955" title="United Kingdom general election, 1955">1955 general election</a>, in later years stating: "I really just felt the twins were ... only two, I really felt that it was too soon. I couldn't do that."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000100_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000100-38">[37]</a></sup> Afterwards, Thatcher began looking for a Conservative <a href="/wiki/Safe_seat" title="Safe seat">safe seat</a> and was selected as the candidate for <a href="/wiki/Finchley_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Finchley (UK Parliament constituency)">Finchley</a> in April 1958 (narrowly beating <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fraser_(Plymouth_Sutton_MP)" title="Ian Fraser (Plymouth Sutton MP)">Ian Montagu Fraser</a>). She was elected as MP for the seat after a hard campaign in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1959" title="United Kingdom general election, 1959">1959 election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200627_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200627-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> Benefiting from her fortunate result in a lottery for <a href="/wiki/Backbencher" title="Backbencher">backbenchers</a> to propose new legislation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-runciman20130606_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-runciman20130606-18">[17]</a></sup> Thatcher's <a href="/wiki/Maiden_speech" title="Maiden speech">maiden speech</a> was in support of her <a href="/wiki/Private_member%27s_bill" title="Private member's bill">private member's bill</a> (<a href="/wiki/Public_Bodies_(Admission_to_Meetings)_Act_1960" title="Public Bodies (Admission to Meetings) Act 1960">Public Bodies (Admission to Meetings) Act 1960</a>), requiring local authorities to hold their council meetings in public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> In 1961 she went against the Conservative Party's official position by voting for the restoration of <a href="/wiki/Birching" title="Birching">birching</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Judicial_corporal_punishment" title="Judicial corporal punishment">judicial corporal punishment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000134_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000134-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher's talent and drive caused her to be mentioned as a future Prime Minister in her early 20s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-runciman20130606_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-runciman20130606-18">[17]</a></sup> although she herself was more pessimistic, stating as late as 1970: "There will not be a woman prime minister in my lifetime – the male population is too prejudiced."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sandbrook20130409_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-sandbrook20130409-43">[42]</a></sup> In October 1961 she was promoted to the front bench as <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Secretary_to_the_Minister_for_Pensions" title="Parliamentary Secretary to the Minister for Pensions">Parliamentary Undersecretary at the Ministry of Pensions and National Insurance</a> in <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a>'s administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan20034_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan20034-44">[43]</a></sup> Thatcher was the youngest woman in history to receive such a post, and among the first MPs elected in 1959 to be promoted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scottsmith2003_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-scottsmith2003-45">[44]</a></sup> After the Conservatives lost the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1964" title="United Kingdom general election, 1964">1964 election</a> she became spokesman on Housing and Land, in which position she advocated her party's policy of allowing tenants to buy their <a href="/wiki/Council_house" title="Council house">council houses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764-46">[45]</a></sup> She moved to the Shadow <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">Treasury</a> team in 1966 and, as Treasury spokesman, opposed Labour's mandatory price and income controls, arguing that they would produce effects contrary to those intended and distort the economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764-46">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1966, party leaders viewed Thatcher as a potential <a href="/wiki/Official_Opposition_Shadow_Cabinet_(United_Kingdom)" title="Official Opposition Shadow Cabinet (United Kingdom)">Shadow Cabinet</a> member. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Prior" title="James Prior">James Prior</a> proposed her as a member after the Conservatives' <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1966" title="United Kingdom general election, 1966">1966 defeat</a>, but party leader <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chief_Whip" title="Chief Whip">Chief Whip</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Willie_Whitelaw" title="Willie Whitelaw">Willie Whitelaw</a> chose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mervyn_Pike" title="Mervyn Pike">Mervyn Pike</a> as the shadow cabinet's sole woman member.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scottsmith2003_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-scottsmith2003-45">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the Conservative Party conference of 1966 she criticised the high-tax policies of the Labour government as being steps "not only towards Socialism, but towards Communism", arguing that lower taxes served as an incentive to hard work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764-46">[45]</a></sup> Thatcher was one of the few Conservative MPs to support <a href="/wiki/Leo_Abse" title="Leo Abse">Leo Abse</a>'s Bill to decriminalise male homosexuality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> She voted in favour of <a href="/wiki/David_Steel" title="David Steel">David Steel</a>'s bill to legalise abortion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995150_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995150-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> as well as a ban on <a href="/wiki/Hare_coursing" title="Hare coursing">hare coursing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> She supported the retention of capital punishment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> and voted against the relaxation of divorce laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995151_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995151-53">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1967, the <a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_the_United_States,_London" title="Embassy of the United States, London">United States Embassy in London</a> chose Thatcher to take part in the <a href="/wiki/International_Visitor_Leadership_Program" title="International Visitor Leadership Program">International Visitor Leadership Program</a> (then called the Foreign Leader Program), a professional exchange programme that gave her the opportunity to spend about six weeks visiting various US cities and political figures as well as institutions such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>. Although she was not yet a cabinet or shadow cabinet member, the embassy reportedly described her to the State Department as a possible future Prime Minister. The description helped Thatcher meet with many prominent people during a busy itinerary focused on economic issues, including <a href="/wiki/Paul_Samuelson" title="Paul Samuelson">Paul Samuelson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Whitman_Rostow" title="Walt Whitman Rostow">Walt Rostow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Paul_Schweitzer" title="Pierre-Paul Schweitzer">Pierre-Paul Schweitzer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Rockefeller" title="Nelson Rockefeller">Nelson Rockefeller</a>. After Pike's retirement, Heath appointed Thatcher later that year to the Shadow Cabinet<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scottsmith2003_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-scottsmith2003-45">[44]</a></sup> as Fuel and Power spokesman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Shortly before the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1970" title="United Kingdom general election, 1970">1970 general election</a>, she was promoted to Shadow Transport spokesman and later to Education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200765_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200765-55">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_Secretary:_1970.E2.80.931974">Education Secretary: 1970–1974</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">"Milk Snatcher" redirects here. For the amended School Milk Act that fomented this nickname, see <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1944" title="Education Act 1944">Education Act 1944</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="782" data-file-width="800" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg/220px-Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg/330px-Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg/440px-Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Margaret Thatcher abolished junior school milk in 1970.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Conservative Party under <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> won the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1970" title="United Kingdom general election, 1970">1970 general election</a>, and Thatcher was subsequently appointed to the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">Cabinet</a> as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Education" title="Secretary of State for Education">Secretary of State for Education and Science</a>. During her first months in office she attracted public attention as a result of the administration's attempts to cut spending. She gave priority to academic needs in schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200314_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200314-56">[55]</a></sup> She imposed public expenditure cuts on the state education system, resulting in the abolition of free milk for schoolchildren aged seven to eleven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776-57">[56]</a></sup> She held that few children would suffer if schools were charged for milk, but agreed to provide younger children with a third of a pint daily, for nutritional purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776-57">[56]</a></sup> Cabinet papers later revealed that she opposed the policy but had been forced into it by the Treasury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Her decision provoked a storm of protest from Labour and the press,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200315_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200315-59">[58]</a></sup> leading to the moniker "Margaret Thatcher, Milk Snatcher".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> She reportedly considered leaving politics in the aftermath and later wrote in her autobiography: "I learned a valuable lesson [from the experience]. I had incurred the maximum of political odium for the minimum of political benefit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200315_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200315-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995182_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995182-61">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher supported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victor_Rothschild" title="Victor Rothschild">Lord Rothschild</a>'s 1971 proposal for market forces to affect government funding of research. Although many scientists opposed the proposal, her research background probably made her sceptical of their claim that outsiders should not interfere with funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lecher20130408_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-lecher20130408-17">[16]</a></sup> The department evaluated proposals for more local education authorities to close <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">grammar schools</a> and to adopt <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_school" title="Comprehensive school">comprehensive secondary education</a>. Although Thatcher was committed to a tiered <a href="/wiki/Secondary_modern_school" title="Secondary modern school">secondary modern</a>-grammar school system of education and was determined to preserve grammar schools,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200314_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200314-56">[55]</a></sup> during her tenure as Education Secretary she turned down only 326 of 3,612 proposals for schools to become comprehensives; the proportion of pupils attending comprehensive schools consequently rose from 32 per cent to 62 per cent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007248.E2.80.93249_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007248.E2.80.93249-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leader_of_the_Opposition:_1975.E2.80.931979">Leader of the Opposition: 1975–1979</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Shadow_Cabinet_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Shadow Cabinet of Margaret Thatcher">Shadow Cabinet of Margaret Thatcher</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thatcher-loc.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1913" data-file-width="1606" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Thatcher-loc.jpg/170px-Thatcher-loc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Thatcher-loc.jpg/255px-Thatcher-loc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Thatcher-loc.jpg/340px-Thatcher-loc.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thatcher-loc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
As <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a>, September 1975</div>
</div>
</div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em">
<caption>External audio</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-style: italic">Speech to the <a href="/wiki/National_Press_Club_(United_States)" title="National Press Club (United States)">National Press Club (US)</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="http://www.loc.gov/rr/record/pressclub/thatcher.html" rel="nofollow">Thatcher's speech on 19 September 1975</a> (starts at 7:39, finishes at 28:33)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npc_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-npc-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The Heath government continued to experience difficulties with <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">oil embargoes</a> and union demands for wage increases in 1973 and lost the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_February_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, February 1974">February 1974 general election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200315_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200315-59">[58]</a></sup> Labour formed a minority government and went on to win a narrow majority in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_October_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, October 1974">October 1974 general election</a>. Heath's <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Conservative Party (UK)">leadership of the Conservative Party</a> looked increasingly in doubt. Thatcher was not initially the obvious replacement, but she eventually became the main challenger, promising a fresh start.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200316_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200316-65">[64]</a></sup> Her main support came from the Conservative <a href="/wiki/1922_Committee" title="1922 Committee">1922 Committee</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200316_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200316-65">[64]</a></sup> but Thatcher's time in office gave her the reputation of a pragmatist instead of an ideologue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-runciman20130606_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-runciman20130606-18">[17]</a></sup> She <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1975" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1975">defeated Heath</a> on the first ballot and he resigned the leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> In the second ballot she defeated Whitelaw, Heath's preferred successor. The vote polarised along right-left lines, with the region, experience and education of the MP also having their effects. Thatcher's support was stronger among MPs on the right, those from southern England, and those who had not attended public schools or <a href="/wiki/Oxbridge" title="Oxbridge">Oxbridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher became party leader and <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a> on 11 February 1975;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> she appointed Whitelaw as her deputy. Heath was never reconciled to Thatcher's leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher began to attend lunches regularly at the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Economic_Affairs" title="Institute of Economic Affairs">Institute of Economic Affairs</a> (IEA), a think tank founded by the poultry magnate <a href="/wiki/Antony_Fisher" title="Antony Fisher">Antony Fisher</a>, a disciple of <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a>; she had been visiting the IEA and reading its publications since the early 1960s. There she was influenced by the ideas of <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Harris,_Baron_Harris_of_High_Cross" title="Ralph Harris, Baron Harris of High Cross">Ralph Harris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Seldon" title="Arthur Seldon">Arthur Seldon</a>, and she became the face of the ideological movement opposing the <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a>. <a href="/wiki/Keynesian_economics" title="Keynesian economics">Keynesian economics</a>, they believed, was weakening Britain. The institute's pamphlets proposed less government, lower taxes, and more freedom for business and consumers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett2010chapter_11_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett2010chapter_11-70">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The television critic <a href="/wiki/Clive_James" title="Clive James">Clive James</a>, writing in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> during the voting for the leadership, compared her voice of 1973 to a cat sliding down a blackboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[nb 2]</a></sup> Thatcher had already begun to work on her presentation on the advice of <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Reece" title="Gordon Reece">Gordon Reece</a>, a former television producer. By chance Reece met the actor <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">Laurence Olivier</a>, who arranged lessons with the <a href="/wiki/Royal_National_Theatre" title="Royal National Theatre">National Theatre</a>'s voice coach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995267_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995267-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_Vanity_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_Vanity-75">[73]</a></sup> Thatcher succeeded in completely suppressing her Lincolnshire dialect except when under stress, notably after provocation from <a href="/wiki/Denis_Healey" title="Denis Healey">Denis Healey</a> in the House of Commons in April 1983, when she accused the Labour front bench of being <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/frit#Etymology_2" title="wikt:frit">frit</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher wanted to prevent the creation of a Scottish assembly. She told Conservative MPs to vote against the Scotland and Wales Bill in December 1976, which was defeated, and then when new Bills were proposed she supported amending the legislation to allow the English to vote in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_devolution_referendum,_1979" title="Scottish devolution referendum, 1979">1979 referendum on devolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Britain's economy during the 1970s was so weak that Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a> warned his fellow Labour Cabinet members in 1974 of the possibility of "a breakdown of democracy", telling them: "If I were a young man, I would emigrate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett2010chapter_7_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett2010chapter_7-79">[77]</a></sup> In mid-1978, the economy began to improve and opinion polls showed Labour in the lead, with a general election being expected later that year and a Labour win a serious possibility. Now Prime Minister, Callaghan surprised many by announcing on 7 September that there would be no general election that year and he would wait until 1979 before going to the polls. Thatcher reacted to this by branding the Labour government "chickens", and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> leader <a href="/wiki/David_Steel" title="David Steel">David Steel</a> joined in, criticising Labour for "running scared".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Labour government then faced fresh public unease about the direction of the country and a damaging series of strikes during the winter of 1978–79, dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a>". The Conservatives attacked the Labour government's unemployment record, using advertising with the slogan "<a href="/wiki/Labour_Isn%27t_Working" title="Labour Isn't Working">Labour Isn't Working</a>". A <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">general election</a> was called after Callaghan's government lost a <a href="/wiki/1979_vote_of_no_confidence_in_the_government_of_James_Callaghan" title="1979 vote of no confidence in the government of James Callaghan">motion of no confidence</a> in early 1979. The Conservatives won a 44-seat majority in the House of Commons, and Thatcher became the first female British prime minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Iron_Lady">The Iron Lady</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">This section is about a nickname of Thatcher. For the metaphor it is based upon, see <a href="/wiki/Iron_(metaphor)" title="Iron (metaphor)">Iron (metaphor)</a>.</div>
<p>In 1976 her "Britain Awake" foreign policy speech lambasted the Soviet Union, consequently earning her the "Iron Lady" nickname.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britain-awake_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-britain-awake-82">[80]</a></sup> Often regarded as the pre-eminent sobriquet of her career,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> it was inspired by a speech Thatcher gave on foreign affairs and defence policy, with her warning that the Soviets aimed ultimately for "world dominance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britain-awake_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-britain-awake-82">[80]</a></sup> The Soviet Army journal <i><a href="/wiki/Krasnaya_Zvezda" title="Krasnaya Zvezda">Krasnaya Zvezda</a></i> (<i>Red Star</i>) rebutted her stance in a piece entitled "Iron Lady Raises Fears" by Captain Yuri Gavrilov,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gavrilov_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-gavrilov-85">[83]</a></sup> alluding to "Iron Chancellor" <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Bismarck</a> of imperial Germany. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">The Sunday Times</a></i> covered the <i>Red Star</i> article the following day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> and Thatcher went on to embrace the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/epithet" title="wikt:epithet">epithet</a> a week later; in a speech to <a href="/wiki/Finchley_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Finchley (UK Parliament constituency)">Finchley</a> Conservatives she compared it to <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Wellington</a>'s nickname, the "Iron Duke".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup> Thatcher's moniker has since become a <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Iron_Lady_(disambiguation)#Political_leaders" title="Iron Lady (disambiguation)">generic descriptor for strong-willed female politicians</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Premiership_of_the_United_Kingdom:_1979.E2.80.931990">Premiership of the United Kingdom: 1979–1990</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Premiership of Margaret Thatcher">Premiership of Margaret Thatcher</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/First_Thatcher_ministry" title="First Thatcher ministry">First Thatcher ministry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Thatcher_ministry" title="Second Thatcher ministry">Second Thatcher ministry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Third_Thatcher_ministry" title="Third Thatcher ministry">Third Thatcher ministry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg"><img alt="Thatcher is the only woman in a room, where a dozen men in suits sit around an oval table. Reagan and Thatcher sit opposite each other in the middle of the long axis of the table. The room is decorated in white, with drapes, a gold chandelier and a portrait of Lincoln." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="2033" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg/220px-Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg/330px-Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg/440px-Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/List_of_Thatcher_ministers_1979%E2%80%931990" title="List of Thatcher ministers 1979–1990">Thatcher's Cabinet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Ronald_Reagan#Administration_and_Cabinet" title="Presidency of Ronald Reagan">Reagan's Cabinet</a> convene at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>, 1981</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Thatcher became Prime Minister on 4 May 1979. Arriving at Downing Street she said, in a paraphrase of the <a href="/wiki/Prayer_of_Saint_Francis" title="Prayer of Saint Francis">Prayer of Saint Francis</a>: "Where there is discord, may we bring harmony. Where there is error, may we bring truth. Where there is doubt, may we bring faith. And where there is despair, may we bring hope."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_affairs">Domestic affairs</span></h3>
<p>Thatcher was Leader of the Opposition and Prime Minister at a time of increased racial tension in Britain. Commenting on the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_1977" title="United Kingdom local elections, 1977">local elections of May 1977</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> noted "The Tory tide swamped the smaller parties. That specifically includes the <a href="/wiki/National_Front_(UK)" title="National Front (UK)">National Front</a>, which suffered a clear decline from last year".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> Her standing in the polls rose by 11% after a January 1978 interview for <i><a href="/wiki/World_in_Action" title="World in Action">World in Action</a></i> in which she said "the British character has done so much for democracy, for law and done so much throughout the world that if there is any fear that it might be swamped people are going to react and be rather hostile to those coming in", as well as "in many ways [minorities] add to the richness and variety of this country. The moment the minority threatens to become a big one, people get frightened".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> In the 1979 general election, the Conservatives attracted voters from the National Front, whose support almost collapsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200326_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200326-93">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> In a meeting in July 1979 with the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Carington,_6th_Baron_Carrington" title="Peter Carington, 6th Baron Carrington">Lord Carrington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Whitelaw" title="William Whitelaw">William Whitelaw</a> she objected to the number of Asian immigrants, in the context of limiting the number of <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_boat_people" title="Vietnamese boat people">Vietnamese boat people</a> allowed to settle in the UK to fewer than 10,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>As Prime Minister, Thatcher met weekly with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> to discuss government business, and their relationship came under close scrutiny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200328_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200328-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeward2001154_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeward2001154-97">[95]</a></sup> Biographer <a href="/wiki/John_Campbell_(biographer)" title="John Campbell (biographer)">John Campbell</a> says their relations were "punctiliously correct but there was little love lost on either side". The Queen's press secretary leaked anonymous rumours of a rift, which were officially denied by the Palace. Campbell concludes that Thatcher had "an almost mystical reverence for the institution of the monarchy ... Yet at the same time she was trying to modernise the country and sweep away many of the values and practices which the monarchy perpetuated".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup> Thatcher later wrote: "I always found the Queen's attitude towards the work of the Government absolutely correct ... stories of clashes between 'two powerful women' were just too good not to make up."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher199318_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher199318-99">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_taxation">Economy and taxation</span></h4>
<p>Thatcher's economic policy was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Monetarism" title="Monetarism">monetarist</a> thinking and economists such as <a href="/wiki/Milton_Friedman" title="Milton Friedman">Milton Friedman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Walters" title="Alan Walters">Alan Walters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChilds2006185_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChilds2006185-100">[98]</a></sup> Together with <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a>, she lowered direct taxes on income and increased indirect taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200330_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200330-101">[99]</a></sup> She increased interest rates to slow the growth of the money supply and thereby lower inflation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChilds2006185_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChilds2006185-100">[98]</a></sup> introduced cash limits on public spending, and reduced expenditure on social services such as education and housing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200330_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200330-101">[99]</a></sup> Her cuts in higher education spending resulted in her being the first Oxford-educated post-war Prime Minister not to be awarded an honorary doctorate by the University of Oxford, after a 738 to 319 vote of the governing assembly and a student petition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup> Her new centrally funded <a href="/wiki/City_Technology_College" title="City Technology College">City Technology Colleges</a> did not enjoy much success, and the Funding Agency for Schools was set up to control expenditure by opening and closing schools; the <a href="/wiki/Social_Market_Foundation" title="Social Market Foundation">Social Market Foundation</a>, a centre-left think tank, described it as having "an extraordinary range of dictatorial powers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007464_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007464-103">[101]</a></sup></p>

<p>Some Heathite Conservatives in the Cabinet, the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Wets_and_dries" title="Wets and dries">wets</a>", expressed doubt over Thatcher's policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nft_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-nft-106">[104]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1981_England_riots" title="1981 England riots">1981 England riots</a> resulted in the British media discussing the need for a policy U-turn. At the 1980 Conservative Party conference, Thatcher addressed the issue directly, with a speech written by the playwright <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Millar" title="Ronald Millar">Ronald Millar</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJonesKavanaghMoran2007224_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJonesKavanaghMoran2007224-107">[105]</a></sup> that included the lines: "You turn if you want to. <a href="/wiki/The_lady%27s_not_for_turning" title="The lady's not for turning">The lady's not for turning</a>!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nft_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-nft-106">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher's job approval rating fell to 23% by December 1980, lower than recorded for any previous Prime Minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThornton200618_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThornton200618-108">[106]</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/Early_1980s_recession#Recession_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Early 1980s recession">recession of the early 1980s</a> deepened she increased taxes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200331_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200331-109">[107]</a></sup> despite concerns expressed in a statement signed by 364 leading economists issued towards the end of March 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1982, the UK began to experience signs of economic recovery;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFloudJohnson2004392_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFloudJohnson2004392-111">[109]</a></sup> inflation was down to 8.6% from a high of 18%, but unemployment was over 3 million for the first time since the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup> By 1983 overall economic growth was stronger and inflation and mortgage rates were at their lowest levels since 1970, although manufacturing output had dropped by 30% since 1978<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup> and unemployment remained high, peaking at 3.3 million in 1984.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1987, unemployment was falling, the economy was stable and strong, and inflation was low. Opinion polls showed a comfortable Conservative lead, and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_1987" title="United Kingdom local elections, 1987">local council election</a> results had also been successful, prompting Thatcher to call a general election for 11 June that year, despite the deadline for an election still being 12 months away. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1987" title="United Kingdom general election, 1987">election</a> saw Thatcher re-elected for a third successive term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup> Throughout the 1980s revenue from the 90% tax on <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">North Sea oil</a> extraction was used as a short-term funding source to balance the economy and pay the costs of reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007439_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007439-116">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher reformed local government taxes by replacing <a href="/wiki/Rates_(tax)" title="Rates (tax)">domestic rates</a> – a tax based on the nominal rental value of a home – with the <a href="/wiki/Community_Charge" title="Community Charge">Community Charge</a> (or poll tax) in which the same amount was charged to each adult resident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polltax_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-polltax-117">[115]</a></sup> The new tax was introduced in Scotland in 1989 and in England and Wales the following year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200387.E2.80.9388_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200387.E2.80.9388-118">[116]</a></sup> and proved to be among the most unpopular policies of her premiership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polltax_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-polltax-117">[115]</a></sup> Public disquiet culminated in a 70,000 to 200,000-strong<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trafalgarsq_num_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-trafalgarsq_num-119">[117]</a></sup> demonstration in London on 31 March 1990; the demonstration around <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a> deteriorated into the <a href="/wiki/Poll_Tax_riots" title="Poll Tax riots">Poll Tax riots</a>, leaving 113 people injured and 340 under arrest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-otd_pt_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-otd_pt-120">[118]</a></sup> The Community Charge was abolished by her successor, <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-otd_pt_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-otd_pt-120">[118]</a></sup> It was revealed in December 2016 that Thatcher had herself failed to register for the poll tax and was threatened with a penalty fine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_relations">Industrial relations</span></h4>
<p>Thatcher was committed to reducing the power of the <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Trade unions in the United Kingdom">trade unions</a>, whose leadership she accused of undermining parliamentary democracy and economic performance through strike action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher199397.E2.80.9398.2C_339.E2.80.93340_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher199397.E2.80.9398.2C_339.E2.80.93340-122">[120]</a></sup> Several unions launched strikes in response to legislation introduced to curb their power, but resistance eventually collapsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thatcher-cw_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-thatcher-cw-123">[121]</a></sup> Only 39% of union members voted for Labour in the 1983 general election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup> According to the BBC, Thatcher "managed to destroy the power of the trade unions for almost a generation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcstrike_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcstrike-125">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%9385)" title="UK miners' strike (1984–85)">miners' strike</a> was the biggest confrontation between the unions and the Thatcher government. In March 1984 the <a href="/wiki/National_Coal_Board" title="National Coal Board">National Coal Board</a> (NCB) proposed to close 20 of the 174 state-owned mines and cut 20,000 jobs out of 187,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glass_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glass-126">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Black_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Black-127">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pits-closed_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-pits-closed-128">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two-thirds of the country's miners, led by the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Mineworkers_(Great_Britain)" title="National Union of Mineworkers (Great Britain)">National Union of Mineworkers</a> (NUM) under <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Scargill" title="Arthur Scargill">Arthur Scargill</a>, downed tools in protest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glass_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glass-126">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thatcher-num_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-thatcher-num-129">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-130">[128]</a></sup> Scargill had refused to hold a ballot on the strike,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup> having previously lost three ballots on a national strike (in January 1982, October 1982 and March 1983).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> This led to the strike being declared illegal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher refused to meet the union's demands and compared the miners' dispute to the Falklands conflict two years earlier, declaring in a speech in 1984: "We had to fight the enemy without in the Falklands. We always have to be aware of the enemy within, which is much more difficult to fight and more dangerous to liberty."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhabaz2007226_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhabaz2007226-135">[133]</a></sup> After a year out on strike, in March 1985, the NUM leadership conceded without a deal. The cost to the economy was estimated to be at least £1.5 billion, and the strike was blamed for much of the <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">pound</a>'s fall against the <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US dollar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harper_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harper-136">[134]</a></sup> The government closed 25 unprofitable coal mines in 1985, and by 1992 a total of 97 had been closed;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pits-closed_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-pits-closed-128">[126]</a></sup> those that remained were privatised in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup> The eventual closure of 150 coal mines, not all of which were losing money, resulted in the loss of tens of thousands of jobs and devastated entire communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pits-closed_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-pits-closed-128">[126]</a></sup> Miners had helped bring down the Heath government, and Thatcher was determined to succeed where he had failed. Her strategy of preparing fuel stocks, appointing a union-busting NCB leader in <a href="/wiki/Ian_MacGregor" title="Ian MacGregor">Ian MacGregor</a>, and ensuring police were adequately trained and equipped with riot gear, contributed to her victory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007411_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007411-138">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>The number of stoppages across the UK peaked at 4,583 in 1979, when more than 29 million working days were lost. In 1984, the year of the miners' strike, there were 1,221, resulting in the loss of more than 27 million working days. Stoppages then fell steadily throughout the rest of Thatcher's premiership; in 1990 there were 630 and fewer than 2 million working days lost, and they continued to fall thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButlerButler1994375_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButlerButler1994375-139">[137]</a></sup> Thatcher's time in office witnessed a sharp decline in trade union density, with the percentage of workers belonging to a trade union falling from 57.3% in 1979 to 49.5% in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> In 1979 up until Thatcher's last year in office, trade union membership also fell, from 13.5 million in 1979 to fewer than 10 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200440_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200440-141">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Privatisation">Privatisation</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1502" data-file-width="2131" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg/220px-Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg/330px-Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg/440px-Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Thatcher during a visit to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salford_University" title="Salford University">Salford University</a>, 1982</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The policy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Privatisation" title="Privatisation">privatisation</a> has been called "a crucial ingredient of Thatcherism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeldonCollings200027_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeldonCollings200027-142">[140]</a></sup> After the 1983 election the sale of state utilities accelerated;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeigenbaumHenigHamnett199871_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeigenbaumHenigHamnett199871-143">[141]</a></sup> more than £29 billion was raised from the sale of nationalised industries, and another £18 billion from the sale of council houses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007428_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007428-144">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>The process of privatisation, especially the preparation of nationalised industries for privatisation, was associated with marked improvements in performance, particularly in terms of <a href="/wiki/Workforce_productivity" title="Workforce productivity">labour productivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParkerMartin_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-ParkerMartin-145">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the privatised industries, including gas, water, and electricity, were <a href="/wiki/Natural_monopoly" title="Natural monopoly">natural monopolies</a> for which privatisation involved little increase in competition. The privatised industries that demonstrated improvement sometimes did so while still under state ownership. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Steel_Corporation" title="British Steel Corporation">British Steel Corporation</a>, for instance, made great gains in profitability while still a nationalised industry under the government-appointed chairmanship of Ian MacGregor, who faced down trade-union opposition to close plants and reduce the workforce by half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup> Regulation was also significantly expanded to compensate for the loss of direct government control, with the foundation of regulatory bodies such as <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Gas_and_Electricity_Markets" title="Office of Gas and Electricity Markets">Ofgas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oftel" title="Oftel">Oftel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Rivers_Authority" title="National Rivers Authority">National Rivers Authority</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVeljanovski1990291.E2.80.93304_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVeljanovski1990291.E2.80.93304-147">[145]</a></sup> There was no clear pattern to the degree of competition, regulation, and performance among the privatised industries;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ParkerMartin_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-ParkerMartin-145">[143]</a></sup> in most cases privatisation benefited consumers in terms of lower prices and improved efficiency, but the results overall were "mixed".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcAleese2004169.E2.80.93170_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcAleese2004169.E2.80.93170-148">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher always resisted <a href="/wiki/Privatisation_of_British_Rail" title="Privatisation of British Rail">rail privatisation</a> and was said to have told <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Transport" title="Secretary of State for Transport">Transport Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Ridley,_Baron_Ridley_of_Liddesdale" title="Nicholas Ridley, Baron Ridley of Liddesdale">Nicholas Ridley</a>: "Railway privatisation will be the Waterloo of this government. Please never mention the railways to me again." Shortly before her resignation, she accepted the arguments for privatising <a href="/wiki/British_Rail" title="British Rail">British Rail</a>, which her successor <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> implemented in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007495_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007495-149">[147]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Impact_of_the_privatisation_of_British_Rail" title="Impact of the privatisation of British Rail">effect of privatising the railway</a> is disputed, with large growth in passenger numbers and increasing efficiency<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup> matched by large public subsidy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup> and concern about foreign companies running British railways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>The privatisation of public assets was combined with <a href="/wiki/Deregulation#United_Kingdom" title="Deregulation">financial deregulation</a> in an attempt to fuel economic growth. <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a> abolished Britain's exchange controls in 1979, allowing more capital to be invested in foreign markets, and the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_(financial_markets)" title="Big Bang (financial markets)">Big Bang</a> of 1986 removed many restrictions on the <a href="/wiki/London_Stock_Exchange" title="London Stock Exchange">London Stock Exchange</a>. The Thatcher government encouraged growth in the finance and service sectors to compensate for Britain's ailing manufacturing industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ireland">Northern Ireland</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="758" data-file-width="1000" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg/220px-Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg/330px-Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg/440px-Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Margaret and <a href="/wiki/Denis_Thatcher" title="Denis Thatcher">Denis Thatcher</a> on a visit to <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1980 and 1981, <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a> (IRA) and <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Liberation_Army" title="Irish National Liberation Army">Irish National Liberation Army</a> (INLA) prisoners in Northern Ireland's <a href="/wiki/HM_Prison_Maze" title="HM Prison Maze">Maze Prison</a> carried out <a href="/wiki/1981_Irish_hunger_strike" title="1981 Irish hunger strike">hunger strikes</a> in an effort to regain the status of political prisoners that had been removed in 1976 by the preceding Labour government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strike_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-strike-156">[154]</a></sup></p>
