<p><a href="/wiki/Bobby_Sands" title="Bobby Sands">Bobby Sands</a> began the 1981 strike, saying that he would fast until death unless prison inmates won concessions over their living conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strike_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-strike-156">[154]</a></sup> Thatcher refused to countenance a return to political status for the prisoners, declaring "Crime is crime is crime; it is not political",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strike_156-2"><a href="#cite_note-strike-156">[154]</a></sup> but nevertheless the UK government privately contacted republican leaders in a bid to bring the hunger strikes to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup> After the deaths of Sands and nine others, the strike ended. Some rights were restored to paramilitary prisoners, but not official recognition of political status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAIN-hs_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-CAIN-hs-158">[156]</a></sup> Violence in Northern Ireland escalated significantly during the hunger strikes; in 1982 <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn Féin">Sinn Féin</a> politician <a href="/wiki/Danny_Morrison_(author)" title="Danny Morrison (author)">Danny Morrison</a> described Thatcher as "the biggest bastard we have ever known".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEnglish2005207.E2.80.93208_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEnglish2005207.E2.80.93208-159">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher narrowly escaped injury in an IRA <a href="/wiki/Brighton_hotel_bombing" title="Brighton hotel bombing">assassination attempt</a> at a Brighton hotel early in the morning on 12 October 1984.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc-bomb_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-bomb-160">[158]</a></sup> Five people were killed, including the wife of Cabinet Minister <a href="/wiki/John_Wakeham" title="John Wakeham">John Wakeham</a>. Thatcher was staying at the hotel to attend the Conservative Party conference, which she insisted should open as scheduled the following day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc-bomb_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-bomb-160">[158]</a></sup> She delivered her speech as planned,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher1993379.E2.80.93383_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher1993379.E2.80.93383-161">[159]</a></sup> a move that was widely supported across the political spectrum and enhanced her popularity with the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 6 November 1981, Thatcher and Irish <a href="/wiki/Taoiseach" title="Taoiseach">Taoiseach</a> <a href="/wiki/Garret_FitzGerald" title="Garret FitzGerald">Garret FitzGerald</a> had established the Anglo-Irish Inter-Governmental Council, a forum for meetings between the two governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAIN-hs_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-CAIN-hs-158">[156]</a></sup> On 15 November 1985, Thatcher and FitzGerald signed the Hillsborough <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Agreement" title="Anglo-Irish Agreement">Anglo-Irish Agreement</a>, the first time a British government had given the Republic of Ireland an advisory role in the governance of Northern Ireland. In protest the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Says_No" title="Ulster Says No">Ulster Says No</a> movement attracted 100,000 to a rally in Belfast,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ian_Gow" title="Ian Gow">Ian Gow</a> resigned as <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_State" title="Minister of State">Minister of State</a> in the <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">HM Treasury</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoloney2002336_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoloney2002336-165">[163]</a></sup> and all fifteen Unionist MPs resigned their parliamentary seats; only one was not returned in the subsequent <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_Kingdom_by-elections_(1979%E2%80%932010)#1983.E2.80.931987_Parliament" title="List of United Kingdom by-elections (1979–2010)">by-elections</a> on 23 January 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECochrane2001143_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECochrane2001143-166">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span></h3>
<p>Thatcher supported an active climate protection policy and was instrumental in the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Protection_Act_1990" title="Environmental Protection Act 1990">Environmental Protection Act 1990</a> and in founding the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> and the British <a href="/wiki/Hadley_Centre_for_Climate_Prediction_and_Research" title="Hadley Centre for Climate Prediction and Research">Hadley Centre for Climate Prediction and Research</a> in Exeter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gw_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-gw-167">[165]</a></sup> Thatcher helped to put climate change, acid rain and general pollution in the British mainstream in the early 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gw_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-gw-167">[165]</a></sup> Her speeches included one to Royal Society on 27 September 1988<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup> and to the UN general assembly in November 1989. She did not visit the <a href="/wiki/Earth_Summit" title="Earth Summit">Earth Summit</a> 1992 and later became sceptical about <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_global_warming" title="Politics of global warming">climate change policy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gw_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-gw-167">[165]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_affairs">Foreign affairs</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="551" data-file-width="827" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg/220px-Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg/330px-Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg/440px-Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_Reagan" title="President Reagan">President Reagan</a> and Thatcher at the White House, 16 November 1988</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Thatcher's first foreign policy crisis came with the 1979 Soviet invasion of Afghanistan. She condemned the invasion, said it showed the bankruptcy of a <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9tente" title="Détente">détente</a> policy, and helped convince some British athletes to boycott the 1980 Moscow Olympics. She gave weak support to US President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> who tried to punish the USSR with economic sanctions. Britain's economic situation was precarious, and most of NATO was reluctant to cut trade ties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup> It was reported that her government secretly supplied <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> with military equipment from 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thatcher_Hussein_secret_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thatcher_Hussein_secret-170">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher became closely aligned with the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> policies of United States President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, based on their shared distrust of Communism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thatcher-cw_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-thatcher-cw-123">[121]</a></sup> A more serious disagreement came in 1983 when Reagan did not consult with her on the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Grenada" title="Invasion of Grenada">invasion of Grenada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup> During her first year as Prime Minister she supported <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>'s decision to deploy US nuclear <a href="/wiki/BGM-109G_Ground_Launched_Cruise_Missile" title="BGM-109G Ground Launched Cruise Missile">cruise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pershing_II" title="Pershing II">Pershing II</a> missiles in Western Europe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thatcher-cw_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-thatcher-cw-123">[121]</a></sup> and permitted the US to station more than 160 cruise missiles at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Greenham_Common" title="RAF Greenham Common">RAF Greenham Common</a>, starting on 14 November 1983 and triggering mass protests by the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Nuclear_Disarmament" title="Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament">Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thatcher-cw_123-3"><a href="#cite_note-thatcher-cw-123">[121]</a></sup> She bought the <a href="/wiki/Trident_nuclear_programme" title="Trident nuclear programme">Trident</a> nuclear missile submarine system from the US to replace Polaris, tripling the UK's nuclear forces<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[170]</a></sup> at an eventual cost of more than £12 billion (at 1996–97 prices).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[171]</a></sup> Thatcher's preference for defence ties with the US was demonstrated in the <a href="/wiki/Westland_affair" title="Westland affair">Westland affair</a> of January 1986, when she acted with colleagues to allow the struggling helicopter manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Westland_Helicopters" title="Westland Helicopters">Westland</a> to refuse a takeover offer from the Italian firm <a href="/wiki/Agusta" title="Agusta">Agusta</a> in favour of the management's preferred option, a link with <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_Aircraft" title="Sikorsky Aircraft">Sikorsky Aircraft Corporation</a>. Defence Secretary <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a>, who had supported the Agusta deal, resigned in protest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007419_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007419-174">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 2 April 1982 the ruling <a href="/wiki/National_Reorganization_Process" title="National Reorganization Process">military junta in Argentina</a> ordered the invasion of the British-controlled <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Georgia" title="South Georgia">South Georgia</a>, triggering the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith198921_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith198921-175">[173]</a></sup> The subsequent crisis was "a defining moment of her [Thatcher's] premiership".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackling2005230_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackling2005230-176">[174]</a></sup> At the suggestion of <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Armstrong,_Baron_Armstrong_of_Ilminster" title="Robert Armstrong, Baron Armstrong of Ilminster">Robert Armstrong</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackling2005230_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackling2005230-176">[174]</a></sup> she set up and chaired a small <a href="/wiki/War_cabinet#Falklands_War.2C_1982" title="War cabinet">War Cabinet</a> (formally called ODSA, Overseas and Defence committee, South Atlantic) to take charge of the conduct of the war,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins198380.E2.80.9381_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins198380.E2.80.9381-177">[175]</a></sup> which by 5–6 April had authorised and dispatched a naval task force to retake the islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins198395_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins198395-178">[176]</a></sup> Argentina surrendered on 14 June and the operation was hailed a success, notwithstanding the deaths of 255 British servicemen and 3 Falkland Islanders. Argentinian deaths totalled 649, half of them after the nuclear-powered submarine <a href="/wiki/HMS_Conqueror_(S48)" title="HMS Conqueror (S48)">HMS <i>Conqueror</i></a> torpedoed and sank the cruiser <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARA_General_Belgrano" title="ARA General Belgrano">ARA <i>General Belgrano</i></a> on 2 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liberation_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-liberation-179">[177]</a></sup> Thatcher was criticised for the neglect of the Falklands' defence that led to the war, and especially by <a href="/wiki/Tam_Dalyell" title="Tam Dalyell">Tam Dalyell</a> in parliament for the decision to sink the <i>General Belgrano</i>, but overall she was considered a highly capable and committed war leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins1983335.E2.80.93336_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins1983335.E2.80.93336-180">[178]</a></sup> The "Falklands factor", an economic recovery beginning early in 1982, and a bitterly divided opposition all contributed to Thatcher's second election victory in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">1983</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup> Thatcher often referred after the war to the "Falklands Spirit"; journalists <a href="/wiki/Max_Hastings" title="Max Hastings">Max Hastings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simon_Jenkins" title="Simon Jenkins">Simon Jenkins</a> had suggested in 1983 that this reflected her preference for the streamlined decision-making of her War Cabinet over the painstaking deal-making of peacetime <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">cabinet government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins1983329_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins1983329-182">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 1982 she visited China to discuss with <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a> the <a href="/wiki/Transfer_of_sovereignty_over_Hong_Kong" title="Transfer of sovereignty over Hong Kong">sovereignty of Hong Kong</a> after 1997. China was the first communist state Thatcher had visited and she was the first British prime minister to visit China. Throughout their meeting, she sought the PRC's agreement to a continued British presence in the territory. Deng stated that the PRC's sovereignty on Hong Kong was non-negotiable, but he was willing to settle the sovereignty issue with Britain through formal negotiations, and both governments promised to maintain Hong Kong's stability and prosperity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yahuda_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yahuda-183">[181]</a></sup> After the two-year negotiations, Thatcher conceded to the PRC government and signed the <a href="/wiki/Sino-British_Joint_Declaration" title="Sino-British Joint Declaration">Sino-British Joint Declaration</a> in Beijing in 1984, agreeing to hand over Hong Kong's sovereignty in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan2003116_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan2003116-184">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although saying that she was in favour of "peaceful negotiations" to end <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup> Thatcher stood against the sanctions imposed on South Africa by the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">EC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2011322_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2011322-186">[184]</a></sup> She attempted to preserve trade with South Africa while persuading the government there to abandon apartheid. This included "[c]asting herself as President <a href="/wiki/P._W._Botha" title="P. W. Botha">Botha</a>'s candid friend", and inviting him to visit the UK in June 1984, in spite of the "inevitable demonstrations" against his government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2011325_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2011325-187">[185]</a></sup> Thatcher dismissed the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a> (ANC) in October 1987 as "a typical terrorist organisation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe1994477.E2.80.93478_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe1994477.E2.80.93478-189">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Thatcher government supported the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Rouge" title="Khmer Rouge">Khmer Rouge</a> keeping their seat in the UN after they were ousted from power in Cambodia by the <a href="/wiki/Cambodian%E2%80%93Vietnamese_War" title="Cambodian–Vietnamese War">Cambodian–Vietnamese War</a>. Although Thatcher denied it at the time, it was revealed in 1991 that from 1983 the <a href="/wiki/Special_Air_Service" title="Special Air Service">SAS</a> was sent to secretly train the "non-Communist" members of the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_Government_of_Democratic_Kampuchea" title="Coalition Government of Democratic Kampuchea">CGDK</a> to fight against the Vietnamese-backed <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Kampuchea" title="People's Republic of Kampuchea">People's Republic of Kampuchea</a> government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newst_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-newst-192">[190]</a></sup> The so-called "non-communist members", the Sihanoukists and the Khmer People's National Liberation Front, "were dominated, diplomatically and militarily, by the Khmer Rouge". <a href="/wiki/Rae_McGrath" title="Rae McGrath">Rae McGrath</a> reported that the SAS had taught "the use of improvised explosive devices, booby traps and the manufacture and use of time-delay devices", in what he called "a criminally irresponsible and cynical policy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newst_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-newst-192">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher and her party had supported British membership of the EC in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum,_1975" title="United Kingdom European Communities membership referendum, 1975">1975 national referendum</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-upi19750604_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-upi19750604-193">[191]</a></sup> but she believed that the role of the organisation should be limited to ensuring free trade and effective competition, and feared that the EC's approach was at odds with her views on smaller government and deregulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESenden20049_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESenden20049-194">[192]</a></sup> Thatcher's antipathy towards <a href="/wiki/European_integration" title="European integration">European integration</a> became more pronounced during her premiership, particularly after her third election victory in 1987. During a 1988 speech in <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a> she outlined her opposition to proposals from the European Community (EC), forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, for a federal structure and increased centralisation of decision making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[193]</a></sup> She said, "We have not successfully rolled back the frontiers of the state in Britain, only to see them re-imposed at a European level, with a European super-state exercising a new dominance from Brussels."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESenden20049_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESenden20049-194">[192]</a></sup> For his part, <a href="/wiki/Enoch_Powell" title="Enoch Powell">Enoch Powell</a> commented that Thatcher's "Bruges Speech" marked, in his view, the 'end of the Community'.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="831" data-file-width="1239" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg/220px-Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg/330px-Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg/440px-Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">President Bush</a> and Thatcher at the beginning of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Desert_Shield" title="Operation Desert Shield">Operation Desert Shield</a>, 2 August 1990</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Thatcher was firmly opposed to the UK's membership of the <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">Exchange Rate Mechanism</a>, a precursor to European monetary union, believing that it would constrain the British economy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecc_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-ecc-196">[194]</a></sup> despite the urging of her <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lawson" title="Nigel Lawson">Nigel Lawson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher1993712_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher1993712-197">[195]</a></sup> but she was persuaded by <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> to join in October 1990, at what proved to be too high a rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007484_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007484-198">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 1986, Thatcher permitted US <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics_F-111_Aardvark" title="General Dynamics F-111 Aardvark">F-111s</a> to use <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> bases for the <a href="/wiki/1986_United_States_bombing_of_Libya" title="1986 United States bombing of Libya">bombing of Libya</a> in retaliation for the alleged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1986_Berlin_discotheque_bombing" title="1986 Berlin discotheque bombing">Libyan bombing of a Berlin discothèque</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cannon_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cannon-199">[197]</a></sup> citing the right of self-defence under <a href="/wiki/Chapter_VII_of_the_United_Nations_Charter" title="Chapter VII of the United Nations Charter">Article 51 of the UN Charter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[nb 3]</a></sup> Polls suggested that fewer than one in three British citizens approved of Thatcher's decision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[200]</a></sup> She was in the US on a state visit when Iraqi leader <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> invaded neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> in August 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gw-pbs_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-gw-pbs-204">[201]</a></sup> During her talks with President <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>, who had succeeded Reagan in 1989, she recommended intervention,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gw-pbs_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-gw-pbs-204">[201]</a></sup> and put pressure on Bush to deploy troops in the Middle East to drive the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Army" title="Iraqi Army">Iraqi Army</a> out of Kuwait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[202]</a></sup> Bush was apprehensive about the plan, prompting Thatcher to remark to him during a telephone conversation that "This was no time to go wobbly!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[203]</a></sup> Thatcher's government provided military forces to the international coalition in the build-up to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, but she had resigned by the time hostilities began on 17 January 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAitken2013600.E2.80.93601_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAitken2013600.E2.80.93601-207">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grice_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-grice-208">[205]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher was one of the first Western leaders to respond warmly to reformist Soviet leader <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>. Following Reagan–Gorbachev summit meetings and reforms enacted by Gorbachev in the USSR, she declared in November 1988 that "We're not in a Cold War now", but rather in a "new relationship much wider than the Cold War ever was".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reforms1988_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-reforms1988-209">[206]</a></sup> She went on a state visit to the Soviet Union in 1984 and met with Gorbachev and <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Ryzhkov" title="Nikolai Ryzhkov">Nikolai Ryzhkov</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Premier_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Premier of the Soviet Union">Chairman</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ministers_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Council of Ministers of the Soviet Union">Council of Ministers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[207]</a></sup> Thatcher was initially opposed to <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">German reunification</a>, telling Gorbachev that it "would lead to a change to postwar borders, and we cannot allow that because such a development would undermine the stability of the whole international situation and could endanger our security". She expressed concern that a united Germany would align itself more closely with the Soviet Union and move away from NATO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEG.C3.B6rtemaker2006198_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEG.C3.B6rtemaker2006198-211">[208]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenges_to_leadership_and_resignation">Challenges to leadership and resignation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1989" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1989">Conservative Party leadership election, 1989</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1990" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1990">Conservative Party leadership election, 1990</a></div>
<p>Thatcher was challenged for the leadership of the Conservative Party by the little-known backbench MP <a href="/wiki/Sir_Anthony_Meyer,_3rd_Baronet" title="Sir Anthony Meyer, 3rd Baronet">Sir Anthony Meyer</a> in the 1989 leadership election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89election_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-89election-212">[209]</a></sup> Of the 374 Conservative MPs eligible to vote, 314 voted for Thatcher and 33 for Meyer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89election_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-89election-212">[209]</a></sup> Her supporters in the party viewed the result as a success, and rejected suggestions that there was discontent within the party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89election_212-2"><a href="#cite_note-89election-212">[209]</a></sup></p>
<p>During her premiership Thatcher had the second-lowest average approval rating, at 40%, of any post-war Prime Minister. Polls consistently showed that she was less popular than her party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ridley_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-ridley-213">[210]</a></sup> A self-described conviction politician, Thatcher always insisted that she did not care about her poll ratings, pointing instead to her unbeaten election record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[211]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1942" data-file-width="1738" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg/170px-Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg/255px-Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg/340px-Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Thatcher inspecting <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermudan</a> troops, 1990</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Opinion polls in September 1990 reported that Labour had established a 14% lead over the Conservatives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howe_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-howe-215">[212]</a></sup> and by November the Conservatives had been trailing Labour for 18 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ridley_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-ridley-213">[210]</a></sup> These ratings, together with Thatcher's combative personality and willingness to override colleagues' opinions, contributed to discontent within the Conservative Party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-resign-nyt_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-resign-nyt-216">[213]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 1 November 1990, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a>, the last remaining member of Thatcher's original 1979 cabinet, resigned from his position as <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Deputy Prime Minister</a> over her refusal to agree to a timetable for Britain to join the <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">European Exchange Rate Mechanism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howe_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-howe-215">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[214]</a></sup> In his resignation speech on 13 November, Howe commented on Thatcher's European stance: "It is rather like sending your opening batsmen to the crease only for them to find the moment that the first balls are bowled that their bats have been broken before the game by the team captain."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[215]</a></sup> His resignation was fatal to Thatcher's premiership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>The next day, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a> mounted a challenge for the leadership of the Conservative Party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007473_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007473-220">[217]</a></sup> Opinion polls had indicated that he would give the Conservatives a national lead over Labour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[218]</a></sup> Although Thatcher won the first ballot with 204 to 152 votes and 16 abstentions, Heseltine had attracted sufficient support to force a second ballot. Under party rules, Thatcher not only needed to win a majority, but her margin over Heseltine had to be equivalent to 15% of the 372 Conservative MPs in order to win the leadership election outright; with 54.8% against 40.9% for Heseltine, she came up four votes short.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-number-10_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-number-10-222">[219]</a></sup> Thatcher initially stated that she intended to "fight on and fight to win" the second ballot, but consultation with her Cabinet persuaded her to withdraw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-resign-nyt_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-resign-nyt-216">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-resign-bbc_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-resign-bbc-223">[220]</a></sup> After visiting the Queen, calling other world leaders, and making one final Commons speech,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[221]</a></sup> she left Downing Street in tears. She reportedly regarded her ousting as a betrayal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007474_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007474-225">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>The resignation surprised <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[223]</a></sup> Thatcher was replaced as Prime Minister and party leader by her Chancellor <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>, who prevailed over Heseltine in the subsequent ballot. Major oversaw an upturn in Conservative support in the 17 months leading up to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1992" title="United Kingdom general election, 1992">1992 general election</a> and led the Conservatives to their fourth successive victory on 9 April 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[224]</a></sup> Thatcher had favoured Major over Heseltine in the leadership contest, but her support for him weakened in later years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[225]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span></h2>
<p>Thatcher returned to the <a href="/wiki/Backbencher" title="Backbencher">backbenches</a> as MP for Finchley for two years after leaving the premiership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan2003118_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan2003118-229">[226]</a></sup> She retired from the House at the 1992 election, aged 66, saying that leaving the Commons would allow her more freedom to speak her mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lords_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-lords-230">[227]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Commons:_1992.E2.80.932003">Post-Commons: 1992–2003</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1452" data-file-width="1360" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Margaret_Thatcher.jpg/170px-Margaret_Thatcher.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Margaret_Thatcher.jpg/255px-Margaret_Thatcher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Margaret_Thatcher.jpg/340px-Margaret_Thatcher.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Thatcher touring the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>, 2001</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After leaving the House of Commons, Thatcher became the first former Prime Minister to set up a foundation;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[228]</a></sup> the British wing of the Margaret Thatcher Foundation was dissolved in 2005 because of financial difficulties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[229]</a></sup> She wrote two volumes of memoirs, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Downing_Street_Years" title="The Downing Street Years">The Downing Street Years</a></i> (1993) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Path_to_Power_(Margaret_Thatcher)" title="The Path to Power (Margaret Thatcher)">The Path to Power</a></i> (1995). In 1991, she and her husband Denis moved to a house in <a href="/wiki/Chester_Square" title="Chester Square">Chester Square</a>, a residential garden square in central London's <a href="/wiki/Belgravia" title="Belgravia">Belgravia</a> district.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[230]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1992, Thatcher was hired by the tobacco company <a href="/wiki/Altria" title="Altria">Philip Morris</a> as a "geopolitical consultant" for $250,000 per year and an annual contribution of $250,000 to her foundation. She also earned $50,000 for each speech she delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[231]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 1992, Thatcher called for NATO to stop the Serbian assault on <a href="/wiki/Gora%C5%BEde" title="Goražde">Goražde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a> to end <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnian War</a>. She compared the situation in Bosnia to "the worst excesses of the Nazis", and warned that there could be a "holocaust".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[232]</a></sup> She had been an advocate of <a href="/wiki/Independence_of_Croatia" title="Independence of Croatia">Croatian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independence_of_Slovenia" title="Independence of Slovenia">Slovenian</a> independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[233]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 1991 interview for <a href="/wiki/Croatian_Radiotelevision" title="Croatian Radiotelevision">Croatian Radiotelevision</a>, Thatcher had commented on the <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_Wars" title="Yugoslav Wars">Yugoslav Wars</a>; she was critical of Western governments for not recognising the breakaway republics of Croatia and Slovenia as independent states and for not supplying them with arms after the Serbian-led <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_People%27s_Army" title="Yugoslav People's Army">Yugoslav Army</a> attacked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p>She made a series of speeches in the Lords criticising the <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lords_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-lords-230">[227]</a></sup> describing it as "a treaty too far" and stated: "I could never have signed this treaty."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[235]</a></sup> She cited <a href="/wiki/A._V._Dicey" title="A. V. Dicey">A. V. Dicey</a> when stating that as all three main parties were in favour of the treaty, the people should have their say in a referendum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[236]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher was honorary <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_(education)" title="Chancellor (education)">Chancellor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="College of William &amp; Mary">College of William &amp; Mary</a> in Virginia from 1993 to 2000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[237]</a></sup> and also of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Buckingham" title="University of Buckingham">University of Buckingham</a> from 1992 to 1999, the UK's first private university, which she had opened in 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[238]</a></sup></p>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1994" title="Labour Party (UK) leadership election, 1994">election as Labour Party leader</a> in 1994, Thatcher praised Blair in an interview as "probably the most formidable Labour leader since <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Gaitskell" title="Hugh Gaitskell">Hugh Gaitskell</a>. I see a lot of socialism behind their front bench, but not in Mr Blair. I think he genuinely has moved".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[239]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1998, Thatcher called for the release of former Chilean dictator <a href="/wiki/Augusto_Pinochet" title="Augusto Pinochet">Augusto Pinochet</a> when Spain had him <a href="/wiki/Indictment_and_arrest_of_Augusto_Pinochet" title="Indictment and arrest of Augusto Pinochet">arrested</a> and sought to try him for human rights violations. She cited the help he gave Britain during the Falklands War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[240]</a></sup> In 1999, she visited him while he was under house arrest near London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[241]</a></sup> Pinochet was released in March 2000 on medical grounds by Home Secretary <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw" title="Jack Straw">Jack Straw</a>, without facing trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2001" title="United Kingdom general election, 2001">2001 general election</a>, Thatcher supported the Conservative general election campaign, as she had done in 1992 and 1997, and in the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_2001" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 2001">Conservative leadership election</a> shortly after, she supported <a href="/wiki/Iain_Duncan_Smith" title="Iain Duncan Smith">Iain Duncan Smith</a> over <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Clarke" title="Kenneth Clarke">Kenneth Clarke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002, Thatcher encouraged President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> to aggressively tackle the "unfinished business" of <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a>'s Iraq,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thatcher_NYT_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thatcher_NYT-247">[244]</a></sup> and praised <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> for his "strong, bold leadership" in standing with Bush in the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thatcher_Blair_Iraq_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thatcher_Blair_Iraq-248">[245]</a></sup> Later that year Thatcher was said to have regarded Blair and "<a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a>" as her greatest achievement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>She broached the same subject in her <i><a href="/wiki/Statecraft:_Strategies_for_a_Changing_World" title="Statecraft: Strategies for a Changing World">Statecraft: Strategies for a Changing World</a></i>, which was published that year and dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, writing that there would be no peace in the Middle East until <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> was toppled. Her book also said that Israel must trade land for peace, and that the European Union (EU) was "fundamentally unreformable", "a classic utopian project, a monument to the vanity of intellectuals, a programme whose inevitable destiny is failure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[247]</a></sup> She argued that Britain should renegotiate its terms of membership or else leave the EU and join the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">North American Free Trade Area</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[248]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following several small strokes she was advised by her doctors not to engage in further public speaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[249]</a></sup> On 23 March 2002, she announced that on the advice of her doctors she would cancel all planned speaking engagements and accept no more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2003796.E2.80.93798_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2003796.E2.80.93798-253">[250]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Husband.27s_death:_2003">Husband's death: 2003</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Denis_Thatcher" title="Denis Thatcher">Sir Denis Thatcher</a> died of <a href="/wiki/Pancreatic_cancer" title="Pancreatic cancer">pancreatic cancer</a> on 26 June 2003 and was cremated on 3 July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[251]</a></sup> She had paid tribute to him in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Downing_Street_Years" title="The Downing Street Years">The Downing Street Years</a></i>, writing "Being Prime Minister is a lonely job. In a sense, it ought to be: you cannot lead from the crowd. But with Denis there I was never alone. What a man. What a husband. What a friend".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher199323_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher199323-255">[252]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years:_2003.E2.80.932013">Final years: 2003–2013</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="343" data-file-width="515" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg/220px-Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg/330px-Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg/440px-Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Thatcher with former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Yasuhiro_Nakasone" title="Yasuhiro Nakasone">Yasuhiro Nakasone</a> of Japan (far left), <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Mulroney" title="Brian Mulroney">Brian Mulroney</a> of Canada (centre) attend Reagan's funeral, 11 June 2004</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 11 June 2004, Thatcher, against doctor's orders, attended the <a href="/wiki/Death_and_state_funeral_of_Ronald_Reagan" title="Death and state funeral of Ronald Reagan">state funeral service for Ronald Reagan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[253]</a></sup> She delivered her eulogy via videotape; in view of her health, the message had been pre-recorded several months earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[255]</a></sup> Thatcher flew to <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> with the Reagan entourage, and attended the memorial service and interment ceremony for the president at the <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan_Presidential_Library" title="Ronald Reagan Presidential Library">Ronald Reagan Presidential Library</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[256]</a></sup> In early 2005 Thatcher criticised the way the decision to <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">invade Iraq</a> had been made two years previously. Although she still supported the intervention to topple Saddam Hussein, she said that as a scientist, she would always look for "facts, evidence and proof", before committing the armed forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grice_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-grice-208">[205]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher celebrated her 80th birthday at the <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Oriental_Hyde_Park,_London" title="Mandarin Oriental Hyde Park, London">Mandarin Oriental Hotel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park,_London" title="Hyde Park, London">Hyde Park</a>, London, on 13 October 2005; guests included the Queen, the <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" title="Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh">Duke of Edinburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princess_Alexandra,_The_Honourable_Lady_Ogilvy" title="Princess Alexandra, The Honourable Lady Ogilvy">Princess Alexandra</a> and Tony Blair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[257]</a></sup> Geoffrey Howe, by then Lord Howe of Aberavon, was also present, and said of his former leader: "Her real triumph was to have transformed not just one party but two, so that when Labour did eventually return, the great bulk of Thatcherism was accepted as irreversible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[258]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a later article in <i>The Daily Telegraph</i>, Thatcher's daughter Carol <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikinews.org/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher_suffering_from_dementia,_says_daughter" title="n:Margaret Thatcher suffering from dementia, says daughter">first revealed</a> that her mother had <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">dementia</a> in 2005, saying that "Mum doesn't read much any more because of her memory loss ... It's pointless. She can't remember the beginning of a sentence by the time she reaches the end".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langley_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-Langley-262">[259]</a></sup> She later recounted how she was first struck by her mother's dementia when in conversation Thatcher confused the Falklands and Yugoslav conflicts; she recalled the pain of needing to tell her mother repeatedly that Denis Thatcher was dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dem-cbs_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-dem-cbs-263">[260]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="343" data-file-width="515" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg/220px-Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg/330px-Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg/440px-Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Thatcher attends a Washington memorial service marking the fifth anniversary of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/9/11" title="9/11">9/11</a> attacks, pictured with Vice President <a href="/wiki/Dick_Cheney" title="Dick Cheney">Dick Cheney</a> and his wife</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2006, Thatcher attended the official Washington, D.C. memorial service to commemorate the fifth anniversary of the 2001 <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">11 September attacks</a> on the US. She was a guest of Vice President <a href="/wiki/Dick_Cheney" title="Dick Cheney">Dick Cheney</a>, and met Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Condoleezza_Rice" title="Condoleezza Rice">Condoleezza Rice</a> during her visit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[261]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2007, Thatcher became the first living British prime minister to be honoured with a <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Margaret_Thatcher,_Palace_of_Westminster" title="Statue of Margaret Thatcher, Palace of Westminster">statue in the Houses of Parliament</a>. The bronze statue stands opposite that of her political hero <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Winston_Churchill,_Palace_of_Westminster" title="Statue of Winston Churchill, Palace of Westminster">Sir Winston Churchill's</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bronze_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-bronze-265">[262]</a></sup> and was unveiled on 21 February 2007 with Thatcher in attendance; she made a brief speech in the members' lobby of the House of Commons, responding: "I might have preferred iron – but bronze will do ... It won't rust."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bronze_265-1"><a href="#cite_note-bronze-265">[262]</a></sup></p>
<p>She was a public supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Prague_Declaration_on_European_Conscience_and_Communism" title="Prague Declaration on European Conscience and Communism">Prague Declaration on European Conscience and Communism</a> and the resulting Prague Process, and sent a public letter of support to its preceding conference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[263]</a></sup></p>
<p>After collapsing at a House of Lords dinner, Thatcher was admitted to <a href="/wiki/St_Thomas%27_Hospital" title="St Thomas' Hospital">St Thomas' Hospital</a> in central London on 7 March 2008 for tests. In 2009 she was hospitalised again when she fell and broke her arm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[264]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher returned to 10 Downing Street in late November 2009 for the unveiling of an official portrait by artist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Stone_(painter)" title="Richard Stone (painter)">Richard Stone</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThatcherReturns_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-ThatcherReturns-268">[265]</a></sup> an unusual honour for a living ex-Prime Minister. Stone had previously painted portraits of the Queen and the <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_The_Queen_Mother" title="Queen Elizabeth The Queen Mother">Queen Mother</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThatcherReturns_268-1"><a href="#cite_note-ThatcherReturns-268">[265]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 4 July 2011, Thatcher was to attend a ceremony for the unveiling of a 10 ft (3.0 m) statue to former US President Ronald Reagan, outside the <a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_the_United_States,_London" title="Embassy of the United States, London">American Embassy</a> in London, but was unable to attend due to her frail health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[266]</a></sup> On 31 July 2011, it was announced that her office in the House of Lords had been closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[267]</a></sup> Earlier that month, Thatcher had been named the most competent British prime minister of the past 30 years in an <a href="/wiki/Ipsos_MORI" title="Ipsos MORI">Ipsos MORI</a> poll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[268]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_funeral:_2013">Death and funeral: 2013</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Death_and_funeral_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Death and funeral of Margaret Thatcher">Death and funeral of Margaret Thatcher</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1959" data-file-width="3060" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg/220px-Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg/330px-Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg/440px-Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Thatcher's coffin being carried up the steps of <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul's Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Baroness Thatcher died on 8 April 2013, at the age of 87, after suffering a stroke. She had been staying at a suite in <a href="/wiki/The_Ritz_Hotel,_London" title="The Ritz Hotel, London">the Ritz Hotel</a> in London since December 2012 after having difficulty with stairs at her <a href="/wiki/Chester_Square" title="Chester Square">Chester Square</a> home in <a href="/wiki/Belgravia" title="Belgravia">Belgravia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[269]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Death_and_funeral_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="q:Death and funeral of Margaret Thatcher">Reactions to the news of Thatcher's death</a> were mixed in the UK, ranging from tributes lauding her as Britain's greatest-ever peacetime Prime Minister to public celebrations of her death and expressions of personalised vitriol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[270]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MTgrave1.jpeg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/MTgrave1.jpeg/170px-MTgrave1.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/MTgrave1.jpeg/255px-MTgrave1.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/MTgrave1.jpeg/340px-MTgrave1.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MTgrave1.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Plaques on the graves of Margaret and Denis Thatcher at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Hospital_Chelsea" title="Royal Hospital Chelsea">Royal Hospital Chelsea</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Details of Thatcher's funeral had been agreed with her in advance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Independent.2C_12_April_2013_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-Independent.2C_12_April_2013-274">[271]</a></sup> She received a <a href="/wiki/State_funerals_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="State funerals in the United Kingdom">ceremonial funeral</a>, including full military honours, with a church service at <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul's Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a> on 17 April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-275">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News.2C_9_April_2013_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News.2C_9_April_2013-276">[273]</a></sup></p>
<p>Queen Elizabeth II and Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh, attended the funeral,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[274]</a></sup> the second time in the Queen's reign that she had attended the funeral of a former Prime Minister (the first being that of <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> in 1965).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[275]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the service at St Paul's Cathedral, Thatcher's body was cremated at <a href="/wiki/Mortlake_Crematorium" title="Mortlake Crematorium">Mortlake Crematorium</a>, where her husband had been cremated. On 28 September a service for Thatcher was held in the All Saints Chapel of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Hospital_Chelsea" title="Royal Hospital Chelsea">Royal Hospital Chelsea</a>'s Margaret Thatcher Infirmary. In a private ceremony Thatcher's ashes were interred in the grounds of the hospital, next to those of her husband.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telegraph-20130913_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph-20130913-279">[276]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-intere_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-intere-280">[277]</a></sup></p>

<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Thatcherism" title="Template:Thatcherism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Thatcherism" title="Template talk:Thatcherism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Thatcherism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
