</table>
<p>Historian Stanislao Pugliese argues that Thatcher "looms as one of the major figures of 20th-century history" and notes that "few politicians generate the amount of admiration and indignation as does Margaret Thatcher. Fewer still have left as profound a mark on their country or the international scene".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[278]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcherism represented a systematic, decisive rejection and reversal of the <a href="/wiki/Post-war_consensus" title="Post-war consensus">post-war consensus</a>, whereby the major political parties largely agreed on the central themes of Keynesianism, the welfare state, nationalised industry, and close regulation of the economy. The <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> (NHS) was a notable exception; she promised in 1982 that it was "safe in our hands".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[279]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher defined her own political philosophy in a major and controversial break with <a href="/wiki/One-nation_conservatism" title="One-nation conservatism">One Nation Conservatives</a> like her predecessor Edward Heath,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[280]</a></sup> in her statement to Douglas Keay, published in <i><a href="/wiki/Woman%27s_Own" title="Woman's Own">Woman's Own</a></i> magazine in September 1987:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>What is wrong with the deterioration? [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] I think we have gone through a period when too many children and people have been given to understand "I have a problem, it is the Government's job to cope with it!" or "I have a problem, I will go and get a grant to cope with it!" "I am homeless, the Government must house me!" and so they are casting their problems on society and who is society? There is no such thing! There are individual men and women and there are families and no government can do anything except through people and people look to themselves first. It is our duty to look after ourselves and then also to help look after our neighbour and life is a reciprocal business and people have got the entitlements too much in mind without the obligations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[281]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The number of adults owning shares rose from 7 per cent to 25 per cent during her tenure, and more than a million families bought their council houses, giving an increase from 55 per cent to 67 per cent in owner occupiers from 1979 to 1990. The houses were sold at a discount of 33–55 per cent, leading to large profits for some new owners. Personal wealth rose by 80 per cent in real terms during the 1980s, mainly due to rising house prices and increased earnings. Shares in the privatised utilities were sold below their market value to ensure quick and wide sales, rather than maximise national income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007430_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007430-285">[282]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher's premiership was also marked by periods of high unemployment and social unrest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-legacy-bbc_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-legacy-bbc-286">[283]</a></sup> and many critics on the left of the political spectrum fault her economic policies for the unemployment level; many of the areas affected by high unemployment as well as her <a href="/wiki/Monetarism" title="Monetarism">monetarist</a> economic policies remained blighted for decades by social problems such as drug abuse and family breakdown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards200463_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards200463-287">[284]</a></sup> Unemployment did not fall below its 1979 level during her tenure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[285]</a></sup> The long-term effects on manufacturing remain contentious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[286]</a></sup></p>
<p>Speaking in Scotland in April 2009, before the 30th anniversary of her election as Prime Minister, Thatcher insisted she had no regrets and was right to introduce the <a href="/wiki/Community_Charge" title="Community Charge">poll tax</a>, and to withdraw subsidies from "outdated industries, whose markets were in terminal decline", subsidies that created "the culture of dependency, which had done such damage to Britain".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[287]</a></sup> Political economist <a href="/wiki/Susan_Strange" title="Susan Strange">Susan Strange</a> called the new financial growth model "casino capitalism", reflecting her view that speculation and financial trading were becoming more important to the economy than industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGamble200916_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGamble200916-291">[288]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics on the left describe her as "divisive"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greatestpm_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-greatestpm-292">[289]</a></sup> and claim she promoted greed and selfishness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-legacy-bbc_286-1"><a href="#cite_note-legacy-bbc-286">[283]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_White_(journalist)" title="Michael White (journalist)">Michael White</a>, writing in the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i> in February 2009, challenged the view that her reforms had brought a net benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[290]</a></sup> Practically all specialists conclude that despite being the first female Prime Minister, Thatcher did "little to advance the political cause of women",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200425_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200425-294">[291]</a></sup> either within her party or the government. Burns states that some British <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminists</a> regarded her as "an enemy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns2009234_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns2009234-295">[292]</a></sup> June Purvis argues that while she had to struggle hard against the deep sexist prejudices of her day to rise to the top, she made no effort to ease the path for other women. There were some successful women (such as the <a href="/wiki/Spice_Girls" title="Spice Girls">Spice Girls</a>) who in the 1990s praised her as the pioneer of their ideology of girl power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[293]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[294]</a></sup> Thatcher did not regard <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a> as important while Prime Minister, and although she suggested that women should be shortlisted by default for all public appointments, she also proposed that women with children ought to leave the work force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[295]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her stance on immigration in the late 1970s was regarded as part of a rising racist public discourse,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witte_p54_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-witte_p54-299">[296]</a></sup> which the film critic Martin Barker called "<a href="/wiki/New_racism" title="New racism">new racism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChin200992_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChin200992-300">[297]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[298]</a></sup> Sociologists Mark Mitchell and Dave Russell responded that Thatcher had been badly misinterpreted. Race was not an important focus of Thatcherism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[299]</a></sup> The leading racist force in Britain at the time was the <a href="/wiki/National_Front_(UK)" title="National Front (UK)">National Front</a> (NF). She believed that it was winning over large numbers of Conservative voters with warnings against floods of immigrants. Her strategy was to destroy the NF attack by agreeing that many Britons did have serious fears that needed to be addressed. Her shadow home secretary (William Whitelaw) supported the campaign against the NF, but Thatcher as leader successfully opposed the Conservative contributions to the campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[300]</a></sup> In January 1978 Thatcher launched an attack on immigration with the goal of attracting voters away from the NF and back to the Conservatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witte_p54_299-1"><a href="#cite_note-witte_p54-299">[296]</a></sup> In a speech in April 1978 she likened the NF to Communists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[301]</a></sup> Thatcher's rhetoric was followed by the return of Conservative voters from the National Front. The NF peaked in 1978, and collapsed in 1979, never to recover; immigration was not a major political issue in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[302]</a></sup> The Conservative and Labour parties by the 1980s had similar positions on immigration policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[303]</a></sup> In her ten years as Prime Minister there were no policies passed or proposed by the government to restrict immigration. The Conservative government adopted Labour policy recommendations in passing the <a href="/wiki/British_Nationality_Act_1981" title="British Nationality Act 1981">British Nationality Act 1981</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[304]</a></sup> Following her success in taking votes from the NF, the left believed that she was just as racist as the NF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[305]</a></sup> Muhammad Anwar said that Thatcher made no major speeches on race while she was Prime Minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[306]</a></sup></p>
<p>Influenced at the outset by <a href="/wiki/Keith_Joseph" title="Keith Joseph">Keith Joseph</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007358_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007358-310">[307]</a></sup> the term "<a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a>" came to refer to her policies as well as aspects of her ethical outlook and personal style, including <a href="/wiki/Moral_absolutism" title="Moral absolutism">moral absolutism</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_nationalism" title="British nationalism">nationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">interest in the individual</a>, and an uncompromising approach to achieving political goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-eb-311">[308]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[nb 4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher's <a href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom_by_tenure" title="List of Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom by tenure">tenure of 11 years and 209 days</a> as Prime Minister was the longest since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Salisbury" title="Lord Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a> (13 years and 252 days, in three spells) and the longest continuous period in office since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Liverpool" title="Lord Liverpool">Lord Liverpool</a> (14 years and 305 days).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-number-10_222-1"><a href="#cite_note-number-10-222">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[310]</a></sup> She remains the longest-serving Prime Minister officially referred to as such, as the post was only officially given recognition in the <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_precedence_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Orders of precedence in the United Kingdom">order of precedence</a> in 1905, when <a href="/wiki/Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" title="Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a> was in office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[311]</a></sup></p>
<p>She was voted the fourth-greatest British prime minister of the 20th century in a poll of 139 academics organised by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MORI" title="MORI">MORI</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[312]</a></sup> and in 2002 was ranked number 16 in the BBC poll of the <a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_Britons" title="100 Greatest Britons">100 Greatest Britons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[313]</a></sup> In 1999, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine named Thatcher one of the <a href="/wiki/Time_100:_The_Most_Important_People_of_the_Century" title="Time 100: The Most Important People of the Century">100 most important people of the 20th century</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[314]</a></sup> In 2016 she topped <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_4" title="BBC Radio 4">BBC Radio 4</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Woman%27s_Hour" title="Woman's Hour">Woman's Hour</a></i> Power List of women judged to have had the biggest impact on women's lives over the past 70 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[315]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[316]</a></sup> She was named the most influential woman of the past 200 years in December 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[317]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her death in 2013 prompted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactions_to_the_death_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Reactions to the death of Margaret Thatcher">mixed reactions</a>, including reflections of criticism as well as praise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[318]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[319]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[320]</a></sup> Groups celebrated her death in Brixton, Leeds, Bristol and Glasgow,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[321]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[322]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP_Celebrate_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP_Celebrate-327">[323]</a></sup> and a crowd of 3,000 gathered in <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a> to celebrate her demise and protest against her legacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[324]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly after Thatcher's death, Scottish First Minister <a href="/wiki/Alex_Salmond" title="Alex Salmond">Alex Salmond</a> argued that her policies had the "unintended consequence" of encouraging <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_devolution" title="Scottish devolution">Scottish devolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[325]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Foulkes,_Baron_Foulkes_of_Cumnock" title="George Foulkes, Baron Foulkes of Cumnock">Lord Foulkes of Cumnock</a> agreed on <i><a href="/wiki/Scotland_Tonight" title="Scotland Tonight">Scotland Tonight</a></i> that she had provided "the impetus" for devolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[326]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_depictions">Cultural depictions</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Cultural depictions of Margaret Thatcher">Cultural depictions of Margaret Thatcher</a></div>
<p>One of the earliest satires of Thatcher as Prime Minister involved Wells (as writer/performer), <a href="/wiki/Janet_Brown" title="Janet Brown">Janet Brown</a> (voicing Thatcher) and future <i>Spitting Image</i> producer <a href="/wiki/John_Lloyd_(producer)" title="John Lloyd (producer)">John Lloyd</a>, who in 1979 were teamed up by producer <a href="/wiki/Martin_Lewis_(humorist)" title="Martin Lewis (humorist)">Martin Lewis</a> for the satirical audio album <i>The Iron Lady</i> consisting of skits and songs satirising Thatcher's rise to power. The album was released in September 1979, four months after Thatcher became Prime Minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[327]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[328]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher was the subject or the inspiration for 1980s <a href="/wiki/Protest_song" title="Protest song">protest songs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Billy_Bragg" title="Billy Bragg">Billy Bragg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Weller" title="Paul Weller">Paul Weller</a> helped to form the <a href="/wiki/Red_Wedge" title="Red Wedge">Red Wedge</a> collective to support Labour in opposition to Thatcher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Songs_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-Songs-333">[329]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher was lampooned by satirist <a href="/wiki/John_Wells_(satirist)" title="John Wells (satirist)">John Wells</a> in several media. Wells collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Ingrams" title="Richard Ingrams">Richard Ingrams</a> on the spoof "<a href="/wiki/Dear_Bill" title="Dear Bill">Dear Bill</a>" letters, which ran as a column in <i><a href="/wiki/Private_Eye" title="Private Eye">Private Eye</a></i> magazine; they were also published in book form and later became a West End stage revue named <i>Anyone for Denis?</i>, with Wells in the role of Denis Thatcher. The revue was followed by a <a href="/wiki/Anyone_for_Denis%3F_(video)" title="Anyone for Denis? (video)">1982 TV special</a>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Dick_Clement" title="Dick Clement">Dick Clement</a>, in which Thatcher was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Angela_Thorne" title="Angela Thorne">Angela Thorne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[330]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Spitting_Image#Politicians" title="Spitting Image">Spitting Image</a></i>, a British TV show, satirised Thatcher as a bully who ridiculed her own ministers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007417_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007417-335">[331]</a></sup> She was voiced by <a href="/wiki/Steve_Nallon" title="Steve Nallon">Steve Nallon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[332]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher has been depicted in many television programmes, documentaries, films and plays. She was played by <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Hodge" title="Patricia Hodge">Patricia Hodge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ian_Curteis" title="Ian Curteis">Ian Curteis</a>'s long unproduced <i><a href="/wiki/The_Falklands_Play" title="The Falklands Play">The Falklands Play</a></i> (2002) and by <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Riseborough" title="Andrea Riseborough">Andrea Riseborough</a> in the TV film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Long_Walk_to_Finchley" title="The Long Walk to Finchley">The Long Walk to Finchley</a></i> (2008). She is the title character in two films, portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Lindsay_Duncan" title="Lindsay Duncan">Lindsay Duncan</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Margaret_(2009_film)" title="Margaret (2009 film)">Margaret</a></i> (2009) and by <a href="/wiki/Meryl_Streep" title="Meryl Streep">Meryl Streep</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Iron_Lady_(film)" title="The Iron Lady (film)">The Iron Lady</a></i> (2011),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[333]</a></sup> in which she is depicted as suffering from <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">dementia</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[334]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles.2C_awards_and_honours">Titles, awards and honours</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="404" data-file-width="600" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg/220px-Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg/330px-Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg/440px-Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Thatcher receives the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> from President Bush, 1991</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Thatcher became a <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">Privy Councillor</a> (PC) upon becoming Secretary of State for Education and Science in 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[335]</a></sup></p>
<p>She was the first woman entitled to full membership rights as an honorary member of the <a href="/wiki/Carlton_Club" title="Carlton Club">Carlton Club</a> on becoming leader of the Conservative Party in 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[336]</a></sup> Thatcher became an <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institute_of_Chemistry" title="Royal Institute of Chemistry">Honorary Fellow of the Royal Institute of Chemistry</a> (FRIC) in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[337]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher was bestowed with the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Good_Hope" title="Order of Good Hope">Grand Cross of the Order of Good Hope</a>, which was, at that time, the highest existing South African award, in 1991, by President <a href="/wiki/F._W._de_Klerk" title="F. W. de Klerk">F. W. de Klerk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[338]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thatcher was <a href="/wiki/List_of_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_elected_in_1983" title="List of Fellows of the Royal Society elected in 1983">elected a Fellow of the Royal Society</a> (FRS) in 1983, which caused controversy among some of the then-existing Fellows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ns-rs_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-ns-rs-29">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within two weeks of leaving office in 1990, Thatcher was appointed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" title="Member of the Order of Merit">Member of the Order of Merit</a> (OM), an order within the personal gift of the Queen. Her husband, Denis Thatcher, was made a <a href="/wiki/Thatcher_baronets" title="Thatcher baronets">Baronet</a> at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OMBarontecy_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-OMBarontecy-343">[339]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Falklands, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher_Day" title="Margaret Thatcher Day">Margaret Thatcher Day</a> has been marked every 10 January since 1992,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters-January_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-January-344">[340]</a></sup> commemorating her visit to the Falklands in January 1983, six months after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[341]</a></sup></p>
<p>She became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Member_of_the_House_of_Lords" title="Member of the House of Lords">member of the House of Lords</a> in 1992 with a <a href="/wiki/Life_peer" title="Life peer">life peerage</a> as <i>Baroness Thatcher, of Kesteven in the County of Lincolnshire</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lords_230-2"><a href="#cite_note-lords-230">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[342]</a></sup> She was appointed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lady_Companion_of_the_Order_of_the_Garter" title="Lady Companion of the Order of the Garter">Lady Companion of the Order of the Garter</a> (LG), the UK's highest order of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a>, in 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[343]</a></sup></p>
<p>She was a patron of <a href="/wiki/The_Heritage_Foundation" title="The Heritage Foundation">The Heritage Foundation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[344]</a></sup> which established the Margaret Thatcher Center for Freedom in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[345]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In her foreword to the 1979 Conservative party manifesto, Thatcher wrote of "a feeling of helplessness, that a once great nation has somehow fallen behind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The hang-up has always been the voice. Not the timbre so much as, well, the <i>tone</i> – the condescending explanatory whine which treats the squirming interlocutor as an eight-year-old child with personality deficiencies. It has been fascinating, recently, to watch her striving to eliminate this. BBC2 <i>News Extra</i> on Tuesday night rolled a clip from May 1973 demonstrating the Thatcher sneer at full pitch. (She was saying that she wouldn't <i>dream</i> of seeking the leadership.) She sounded like a cat sliding down a blackboard."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames1977119.E2.80.93120_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames1977119.E2.80.93120-72">[71]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-202"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-202">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Speaking to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>, Thatcher stated: "The United States has more than 330,000 members of her forces in Europe to defend our liberty. Because they are here, they are subject to terrorist attack. It is inconceivable that they should be refused the right to use American aircraft and American pilots in the inherent right of self-defence, to defend their own people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[199]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-313"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-313">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lawson" title="Nigel Lawson">Nigel Lawson</a> listed the Thatcherite ideals as: "Free markets, financial discipline, firm control over public expenditure, tax cuts, nationalism, 'Victorian values' (of the Samuel Smiles self-help variety), privatisation and a dash of populism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawson199264_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawson199264-312">[309]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Byrd, Peter, ed. <i>British foreign policy under Thatcher</i> (Philip Allan, 1988).</li>
<li>Gibran, Daniel K. <i>The Falklands War: Britain Versus the Past in the South Atlantic</i> (McFarland, 1997).</li>
<li>Ionescu, Ghita. <i>Leadership in an Interdependent World: The Statesmanship of Adenauer, De Gaulle, Thatcher, Reagan &amp; Gorbachev</i> (1991) 336pp</li>
<li>Lahey, Daniel James. "The Thatcher government's response to the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan, 1979–1980." <i>Cold War History</i> 13#1 (2013): 21–42.</li>
<li>Sharp, Paul, ed. <i>Thatcher's Diplomacy: The Revival of British Foreign Policy</i> (St. Martin's Press, 1997).</li>
<li>Sharp, Paul. "Thatcher's Wholly British Foreign Policy." <i>Orbis: A Journal of World Affairs</i> 35#3 (1991): 395–411.</li>
<li>Turner, Michael J. <i>Britain's international role, 1970–1991</i> (Palgrave Macmillan, 2010).</li>
</ul>

<li>Jackson, Ben and Robert Saunders, eds. <i>Making Thatchers Britain</i> (2012) <a class="external text" href="https://www.amazon.com/Making-Thatchers-Britain-Ben-Jackson/dp/1107683378/" rel="nofollow">excerpt</a></li>
<li>Kowol, Kit. "Renaissance on the Right? New Directions in the History of the Post-War Conservative Party." <i>Twentieth Century British History</i> 27#2 (2016): 290–304.</li>
<li>Marquand, David. "The literature on Thatcher." <i>Contemporary British History</i> 1.3 (1987): 30–31. <a class="external text" href="http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/pdf/10.1080/13619468708580911" rel="nofollow">online</a></li>

</ul>
